%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Flat-top region I proof %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Flat-top overmodulation: region I fundamental component}
    Let
    \begin{equation}
        \underline{s}^{**}(\theta)=s_{\upalpha}^{**}(\theta)+\mathrm{j}s_{\upbeta}^{**}(\theta)
    \end{equation}
    denote the realized normalized voltage vector. Using the six clamping sectors and mirror symmetry around each sector center, the positive-sequence fundamental amplitude is
    \begin{equation}
        m^{(1)}=
        \left|
        \frac{1}{2\pi}\int_{0}^{2\pi}\underline{s}^{**}(\theta)e^{-\mathrm{j}\theta}\,\mathrm{d}\theta
        \right|
        =
        \frac{6}{\pi}\int_{0}^{\pi/6}\Re\!\left\{\underline{s}^{**}(\theta)e^{-\mathrm{j}\theta}\right\}\,\mathrm{d}\theta.
        \label{eq:flat_top_fundamental_half_sector}
    \end{equation}
    In \textbf{region I}, define the clipping onset
    \begin{equation}
        \theta_\mathrm{c}=\frac{\pi}{6}-\gamma,
        \qquad
        \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}<m^*\leq \frac{4}{3}.
    \end{equation}
    Then, on \(0\leq \theta\leq \theta_\mathrm{c}\) the reference is still realizable:
    \begin{equation}
        \underline{s}^{**}(\theta)=m^*e^{\mathrm{j}\theta}
        \qquad \Rightarrow \qquad
        \Re\!\left\{\underline{s}^{**}(\theta)e^{-\mathrm{j}\theta}\right\}=m^*.
    \end{equation}
    On the clipped interval \(\theta_\mathrm{c}\leq \theta\leq \nicefrac{\pi}{6}\), one has
    \begin{equation}
        s_\mathrm{a}^{**}=1, \qquad
        s_\mathrm{c}^{**}=-1, \qquad
        s_\mathrm{b}^{**}=1-\sqrt{3}m^*\cos\!\left(\theta+\frac{\pi}{6}\right).
    \end{equation}
    Applying the Clarke transformation gives
    \begin{equation}
        s_{\upalpha}^{**}(\theta)=\frac{2}{3}+\frac{m^*}{\sqrt{3}}\cos\!\left(\theta+\frac{\pi}{6}\right), \qquad
        s_{\upbeta}^{**}(\theta)=\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}-m^*\cos\!\left(\theta+\frac{\pi}{6}\right).
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Flat-top region I proof cont. %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Flat-top overmodulation: region I fundamental component (cont.)}
    Hence, on the clipped interval
    \begin{equation}
        \Re\!\left\{\underline{s}^{**}(\theta)e^{-\mathrm{j}\theta}\right\}
        =
        s_{\upalpha}^{**}(\theta)\cos\theta+s_{\upbeta}^{**}(\theta)\sin\theta
        =
        \frac{2}{3}\cos\theta+\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}\sin\theta+\frac{m^*}{2}-\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{3}\sin(2\theta).
        \label{eq:flat_top_region1_integrand}
    \end{equation}
    Therefore, the region-I fundamental amplitude follows from
    \begin{equation}
        m^{(1)}=
        \frac{6}{\pi}
        \left[
            \int_{0}^{\theta_\mathrm{c}} m^*\,\mathrm{d}\theta
            +
            \int_{\theta_\mathrm{c}}^{\nicefrac{\pi}{6}}
            \left(
            \frac{2}{3}\cos\theta+\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}\sin\theta+\frac{m^*}{2}-\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{3}\sin(2\theta)
            \right)\mathrm{d}\theta
            \right].
        \label{eq:flat_top_region1_integral}
    \end{equation}
    Evaluating the integrals and using \(\cos\gamma=\nicefrac{2}{\sqrt{3}m^*}\) yields
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{split}
            m^{(1)}
             & =
            m^*
            -\frac{3m^*}{\pi}\gamma
            +\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{\pi}
            -\frac{4}{\pi}
            +\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}\pi m^*}
            +\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{\pi}\sin\gamma,
            \qquad
            \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}<m^*\leq \frac{4}{3} \\
             & =
            m^*
            -\frac{3m^*}{\pi}\arccos\!\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}m^*}\right)
            +\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{\pi}
            -\frac{4}{\pi}
            +\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}\pi m^*}
            +\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{\pi}\sqrt{1-\frac{4}{3(m^*)^2}}.
        \end{split}
        \label{eq:flat_top_region1_closed_form}
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Flat-top overmodulation: transition to full six-step %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Flat-top overmodulation: transition to full six-step}
    In \textbf{region II}, i.e. $m^*>\nicefrac{4}{3}$, phase \(\mathrm{c}\) is clipped over the entire half-sector \(0\leq \theta\leq \nicefrac{\pi}{6}\), while phase \(\mathrm{b}\) is clipped additionally for
    \begin{equation}
        0\leq \theta\leq \theta_\mathrm{b},
        \qquad
        \theta_\mathrm{b}=\gamma-\frac{\pi}{6}.
    \end{equation}
    The \hl{full six-step waveform} is reached when phase \(\mathrm{b}\) is also clipped over the complete half-sector, i.e.
    \begin{equation}
        \theta_\mathrm{b}=\frac{\pi}{6}
        \qquad \Leftrightarrow \qquad
        \gamma=\frac{\pi}{3}.
    \end{equation}
    Inserting this in \eqref{eq:flat_top_gamma_definition} gives the command value at which flat-top PWM reaches six-step operation:
    \begin{equation}
        \arccos\!\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}m^*}\right)=\frac{\pi}{3}
        \qquad \Leftrightarrow \qquad
        m^*=\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}}.
    \end{equation}
    Thus, the following derivation applies to the strict region-II interval
    \begin{equation}
        \frac{4}{3}<m^*<\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}}.
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Flat-top overmodulation: region II fundamental component %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Flat-top overmodulation: region II fundamental component}
    For \(\nicefrac{4}{3}<m^*<\nicefrac{4}{\sqrt{3}}\), the realized vector is piecewise given by
    \begin{equation}
        \underline{s}^{**}(\theta)=
        \begin{cases}
            \nicefrac{4}{3},                                              & 0\leq \theta\leq \theta_\mathrm{b},                 \\[1mm]
            s_{\upalpha}^{**}(\theta)+\mathrm{j}s_{\upbeta}^{**}(\theta), & \theta_\mathrm{b}\leq \theta\leq \nicefrac{\pi}{6},
        \end{cases}
        \label{eq:flat_top_region2_piecewise_vector}
    \end{equation}
    where on the second interval the same expressions as in region I apply, i.e. \eqref{eq:flat_top_region1_integrand}.
    Hence,
    \begin{equation}
        m^{(1)}=
        \frac{6}{\pi}
        \left[
            \int_{0}^{\theta_\mathrm{b}}\frac{4}{3}\cos\theta\,\mathrm{d}\theta
            +
            \int_{\theta_\mathrm{b}}^{\nicefrac{\pi}{6}}
            \left(
            \frac{2}{3}\cos\theta+\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}\sin\theta+\frac{m^*}{2}-\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{3}\sin(2\theta)
            \right)\mathrm{d}\theta
            \right].
        \label{eq:flat_top_region2_integral}
    \end{equation}
    Evaluating \eqref{eq:flat_top_region2_integral} and again using \(\cos\gamma=\nicefrac{2}{\sqrt{3}m^*}\) yields
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{split}
            m^{(1)}
             & =
            m^*
            -\frac{3m^*}{\pi}\gamma
            +\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{\pi}
            -\frac{4}{\pi}
            +\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}\pi m^*}
            +\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{\pi}\sin\gamma,
            \qquad
            \frac{4}{3}<m^*<\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}} \\
             & =
            m^*
            -\frac{3m^*}{\pi}\arccos\!\left(\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}m^*}\right)
            +\frac{\sqrt{3}m^*}{\pi}
            -\frac{4}{\pi}
            +\frac{4}{\sqrt{3}\pi m^*}
            +\frac{2\sqrt{3}}{\pi}\sqrt{1-\frac{4}{3(m^*)^2}}.
        \end{split}
        \label{eq:flat_top_region2_closed_form}
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{Space vector modulation}
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Space vector modulation principle %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Space vector modulation principle}
    \small
    \begin{columns}[T,onlytextwidth]
        \begin{column}{0.45\textwidth}
            Using the elementary vectors from \figref{fig:elementary_voltages_alpha_beta}, define the normalized \hl{dwell factors}
            \begin{equation}
                \tau_j = \frac{t_j}{T_\mathrm{p}}, \qquad j\in\{0,1,\ldots,7\}.
            \end{equation}
            In \hl{space vector modulation (SVM)}, the reference vector is synthesized as a linear combination of elementary vectors weighted by the dwell factors, i.e.,
            \begin{equation}
                \begin{gathered}
                    \bm{s}_{\upalpha\upbeta}^* = \sum_{j=0}^7 \tau_j \bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}j},\\
                    \sum_{j=0}^7 \tau_j = 1, \qquad
                    \tau_j\geq 0.
                \end{gathered}
                \label{eq:SVM_assignment_problem}
            \end{equation}
        \end{column}
        \begin{column}{0.53\textwidth}
            \centering
            \vspace{-0.15cm}
            \begin{figure}
                \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1.25cm,y=1.25cm,>=Latex]
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\R}{2.0}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\tauOne}{0.55}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\tauTwo}{0.35}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\axisFactor}{1.18}
                    \coordinate (O) at (0,0);
                    \foreach \name/\angle in {u1/0,u2/60,u3/120,u4/180,u5/240,u6/300} {
                            \coordinate (\name) at (\angle:\R);
                        }
                    \coordinate (alphaAxisStart) at (180:{\axisFactor*\R});
                    \coordinate (alphaAxisEnd) at (0:{\axisFactor*\R});
                    \coordinate (betaAxisStart) at (270:{\axisFactor*\R});
                    \coordinate (betaAxisEnd) at (90:{\axisFactor*\R});
                    \coordinate (v1) at ($(O)!\tauOne!(u1)$);
                    \coordinate (v2) at ($(O)!\tauTwo!(u2)$);
                    \coordinate (sref) at ($(v1)+(v2)$);

                    \draw[->,thin] (alphaAxisStart) -- (alphaAxisEnd) node[right] {$s_{\upalpha}$};
                    \draw[->,thin] (betaAxisStart) -- (betaAxisEnd) node[left] {$s_{\upbeta}$};
                    \draw[gray!70,thick] (u1) -- (u2) -- (u3) -- (u4) -- (u5) -- (u6) -- cycle;
                    \foreach \vertex in {u1,u2,u3,u4,u5,u6} {
                            \draw[gray!60,->,thin] (O) -- (\vertex);
                        }
                    \draw[signalalpha,->,very thick] (O) -- (v1);
                    \draw[signalbeta,->,very thick] (O) -- (v2);
                    \draw[signalbeta,dashed,thick] (v1) -- (sref);
                    \draw[signalalpha,dashed,thick] (v2) -- (sref);
                    \draw[black,->,very thick] (O) -- (sref);

                    \pgfmathanglebetweenpoints{\pgfpointanchor{O}{center}}{\pgfpointanchor{sref}{center}}
                    \let\sectorAngle\pgfmathresult
                    \node[fill=white, inner sep=1pt, below, xshift=-0.1cm, yshift=-0.1cm] at ($(O)!0.60!(v1)$) {\scriptsize $\tau_1\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}$};
                    \node[fill=white, inner sep=1pt, above left, xshift=-0.01cm, yshift=0.15cm] at ($(O)!0.74!(v2)$) {\scriptsize $\tau_2\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}$};
                    \node[fill=white, inner sep=0.1pt, above left, xshift=-0.1cm] at ($(sref)+(0.03,0.05)$) {$\bm{s}_{\upalpha\upbeta}^*$};
                    \node[fill=white, text=gray!85!black, inner sep=0.1pt, below right] at ($(u1)+(0.02,-0.03)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}$};
                    \node[fill=white, text=gray!85!black, inner sep=0.1pt, above right] at ($(u2)+(0.02,0.03)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}$};
                    \node[fill=white, text=gray!85!black, inner sep=0.1pt, above left] at ($(u3)+(-0.03,0.03)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}3}$};
                    \node[fill=white, text=gray!85!black, inner sep=0.1pt, left] at ($(u4)+(-0.05,0)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}4}$};
                    \node[fill=white, text=gray!85!black, inner sep=0.1pt, below left] at ($(u5)+(-0.03,-0.03)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}5}$};
                    \node[fill=white, text=gray!85!black, inner sep=0.1pt, below right] at ($(u6)+(0.03,-0.03)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}6}$};
                \end{tikzpicture}
                \caption{Reference vector decomposition with exemplary reference vector $\bm{s}_{\upalpha\upbeta}^*$ in the first sector}
            \end{figure}
        \end{column}
    \end{columns}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Dual coordinates %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Dual coordinates}
    \footnotesize
    \begin{columns}[T,onlytextwidth]
        \begin{column}{0.52\textwidth}
            \centering
            \begin{figure}
                \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1.2cm,y=1.2cm,>=Latex]
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\R}{2}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\h}{sqrt(3)}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\tauOne}{0.55}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\tauTwo}{0.35}
                    \coordinate (O) at (0,0);
                    \foreach \name/\angle in {u1/0,u2/60,u3/120,u4/180,u5/240,u6/300} {
                            \coordinate (\name) at (\angle:\R);
                        }
                    \coordinate (tauOneDir) at (1.5,-0.5*\h);
                    \coordinate (tauOneOne) at (1.5,-0.5*\h);
                    \coordinate (tauOneAxisStart) at (-1.80,1.0392);
                    \coordinate (tauOneAxisEnd) at (2.55,-1.4722);
                    \coordinate (tauTwoAxisStart) at (0,-2.45);
                    \coordinate (tauTwoAxisEnd) at (0,2.45);

                    \coordinate (S1) at ($(O)!\tauOne!(u1)$);
                    \coordinate (S2) at ($(O)!\tauTwo!(u2)$);
                    \coordinate (S) at ($(S1)+(S2)$);

                    \coordinate (QtauTwo) at (0,{\tauTwo*\h});
                    \coordinate (QtauOne) at ($(O)!\tauOne!(tauOneOne)$);
                    \coordinate (tauOneMinusHalf) at (-0.75,0.4330);

                    \draw[gray!70,thick] (u1) -- (u2) -- (u3) -- (u4) -- (u5) -- (u6) -- cycle;
                    \draw[->,thick] (-2.35,0) -- (2.75,0) node[right] {$s_\upalpha^*$};
                    \draw[->,thick] (tauTwoAxisStart) -- (tauTwoAxisEnd) node[right] {$s_\upbeta^*$};
                    \draw[->,thick] (tauOneAxisStart) -- (tauOneAxisEnd) node[below right] {$\tau_1$};
                    \node[left] at (tauTwoAxisEnd) {$\tau_2$};

                    \draw[gray!70,dashed] (QtauTwo) -- (S);
                    \draw[gray!70,dashed] (QtauOne) -- (S);
                    \draw[gray!65,thin] (tauOneOne) -- (u1);
                    \draw[gray!65,thin] (tauOneMinusHalf) -- (0,\h);

                    \draw[gray!60,->,thick] (O) -- (u1);
                    \draw[gray!60,->,thick] (O) -- (u2);
                    \draw[signalalpha,->,thick] (O) -- (S1);
                    \draw[signalbeta,->,thick] (O) -- (S2);
                    \draw[->,thick] (O) -- (S);

                    \node[gray!60,, below] at ($(O)!0.82!(u1)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}$};
                    \node[gray!60,above right] at (u2) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}$};
                    \node[signalalpha, below] at ($(O)!0.58!(S1)$) {$\tau_1\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}$};
                    \node[signalbeta, left, fill=white, inner sep=0.25pt] at ($(O)!0.63!(S2)$) {$\tau_2\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}$};
                    \node[above left] at (S) {$\bm{s}_{\upalpha\upbeta}^*$};

                    \node[anchor=east, left] at (-0.22,\h+0.25) {$\tau_2=1$};
                    \draw[thin] (-0.22,\h+0.25) -- (0,\h);
                    \node[anchor=west, left] at (1.15,-1.03) {$\tau_1=1$};
                    \draw[thin] (1.15,-1.03) -- (tauOneOne);
                    \node[anchor=east, left, fill=white, inner sep=0.5pt] at (-0.999,0.38) {$\tau_1=-\frac{1}{2}$};
                    \draw[thin] (-0.999,0.38) -- (tauOneMinusHalf);

                    \node[anchor=west, right] at (0.22,\h+0.25) {$\frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}$};
                    \draw[thin] (0.22,\h+0.25) -- (0,\h);
                    \node[anchor=west, below right] at ($(u1)+(0.1,-0.1)$) {$\frac{4}{3}$};
                    \draw[thin] ($(u1)+(0.1,-0.1)$)  -- (u1);
                \end{tikzpicture}
                \caption{Dual coordinates in first sector}
                \label{fig:dual_coordinates_SVM_first_sector}
            \end{figure}
        \end{column}
        \begin{column}{0.46\textwidth}
            For each sector, the SVM assignment problem \eqref
            {eq:SVM_assignment_problem} can be solved algebraically or geometrically by introducing \hl{dual coordinates} perpendicular to the fundamental vectors of that sector. In sector one, the relevant active vectors form
            \begin{equation}
                \bm{s}_{\upalpha\upbeta}^* = \tau_1\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1} + \tau_2\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}
                \label{eq:dual_coord_sector1_example}
            \end{equation}
            with
            \begin{equation*}
                \bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1} = \begin{bmatrix}\nicefrac{4}{3} & 0\end{bmatrix}^\top,
                \qquad
                \bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2} = \begin{bmatrix}\nicefrac{2}{3} & \nicefrac{2}{\sqrt{3}}\end{bmatrix}^\top.
            \end{equation*}
            From the component-wise representation
            \begin{equation*}
                s_\upalpha^* = \frac{4}{3}\tau_1 + \frac{2}{3}\tau_2,
                \qquad
                s_\upbeta^* = \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}}\tau_2.
            \end{equation*}
            we can derive for the dwell factors
            \begin{equation*}
                \tau_2 = \frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}s_\upbeta^*,
                \qquad
                \tau_1 = \frac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* - \frac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*.
            \end{equation*}
        \end{column}
    \end{columns}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Dwell factors for all sectors %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Dwell factors for all sectors}
    Generalizing the dual coordinate approach to all sectors yields the following active-vector dwell factors table. Moreover, the inactive vectors are applied for the remaining time, i.e.,
    \begin{equation}
        \tau_0+\tau_7 = 1 - \sum_{j=1}^6 \tau_j.
        \label{eq:zero_vector_dwell_time_SVM}
    \end{equation}
    \begin{table}
        \centering
        \setlength{\tabcolsep}{6pt}
        \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.25}
        \begin{tabular}{c c l}
            \toprule
            Sector & \multicolumn{2}{c}{Active-vector dwell factors}                                                                                                                     \\
            \midrule
            $1$    & $\tau_1 = \phantom{-}\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* - \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$ & $\tau_2 = \phantom{-}\nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{2}s_\upbeta^*$                     \\
            $2$    & $\tau_2 = \phantom{-}\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* + \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$ & $\tau_3 = -\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* + \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$ \\
            $3$    & $\tau_4 = -\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* - \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$           & $\tau_3 = \phantom{-}\nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{2}s_\upbeta^*$                     \\
            $4$    & $\tau_4 = -\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* + \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$           & $\tau_5 = -\nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{2}s_\upbeta^*$                               \\
            $5$    & $\tau_6 = \phantom{-}\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* - \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$ & $\tau_5 = -\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* - \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$ \\
            $6$    & $\tau_1 = \phantom{-}\nicefrac{3}{4}s_\upalpha^* + \nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{4}s_\upbeta^*$ & $\tau_6 = -\nicefrac{\sqrt{3}}{2}s_\upbeta^*$                               \\
            \bottomrule
        \end{tabular}
    \end{table}
\end{frame}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Sector detection %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Sector detection}
    \small
    To avoid evaluating a numerically heavy $\theta = \operatorname{atan2}(s_\upbeta^*, s_\upalpha^*)$, to detect the relevant hexagon sector, a simple sign test can be used based on the following table:
    \begin{table}
        \centering
        \setlength{\tabcolsep}{10pt}
        \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.35}
        \begin{tabular}{c c c c}
            \toprule
            Sector & $s_\upbeta^*$ & $\sqrt{3}s_\upalpha^* + s_\upbeta^*$ & $\sqrt{3}s_\upalpha^* - s_\upbeta^*$ \\
            \midrule
            $1$    & $+$           & $+$                                  & $+$                                  \\
            $2$    & $+$           & $+$                                  & $-$                                  \\
            $3$    & $+$           & $-$                                  & $-$                                  \\
            $4$    & $-$           & $-$                                  & $-$                                  \\
            $5$    & $-$           & $-$                                  & $+$                                  \\
            $6$    & $-$           & $+$                                  & $+$                                  \\
            \bottomrule
        \end{tabular}
    \end{table}
    The factors are derived from the geometric condition that the reference vector $\bm{s}_{\upalpha\upbeta}^*$ lies between the two active vectors of the sector.
\end{frame}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Transition diagram of the elementary switching states %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Transition diagram of the elementary switching states}
    One degree of freedom in the SVM assignment problem \eqref{eq:SVM_assignment_problem} is the choice of the elementary vector sequence which can be represented by the below transition diagram.
    \begin{figure}
        \centering
        \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1cm,y=1cm,>=Latex]
            \tikzset{
                transitionedge/.style={signalalpha, line width=0.95pt},
                statelabel/.style={font=\scriptsize, fill=white, inner sep=0.6pt}
            }

            \coordinate (u1) at (2.35,-0.10);
            \coordinate (u2) at (1.10,2.05);
            \coordinate (u3) at (-1.10,2.05);
            \coordinate (u4) at (-2.25,0.35);
            \coordinate (u5) at (-1.25,-1.95);
            \coordinate (u6) at (1.25,-1.95);
            \coordinate (z7) at (0,0.35);
            \coordinate (z0) at (0,-0.10);

            \draw[gray!70,->,thin] (0.10,2.45) -- (0.10,3.15) node[right] {$s_\upbeta$};
            \draw[gray!70,->,thin] (4.15,0.05) -- (5.10,0.05) node[below] {$s_\upalpha$};

            \draw[transitionedge] (u1) -- (u2) -- (u3) -- (u4) -- (u5) -- (u6) -- cycle;
            \draw[transitionedge] (z0) -- (u1);
            \draw[transitionedge] (z0) -- (u3);
            \draw[transitionedge] (z0) -- (u5);
            \draw[transitionedge] (z7) -- (u2);
            \draw[transitionedge] (z7) -- (u4);
            \draw[transitionedge] (z7) -- (u6);

            \foreach \vertex in {u1,u2,u3,u4,u5,u6,z7,z0} {
                    \fill[signaldelta] (\vertex) circle (2.4pt);
                }

            \node[statelabel, anchor=west] at ($(u1)+(0.22,0.10)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}\; +\; -\; -$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=south] at ($(u2)+(0.02,0.18)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}\; +\; +\; -$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=south] at ($(u3)+(-0.05,0.18)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}3}\; -\; +\; -$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=east] at ($(u4)+(-0.18,-0.02)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}4}\; -\; +\; +$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=north] at ($(u5)+(-0.03,-0.20)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}5}\; -\; -\; +$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=north] at ($(u6)+(0.02,-0.20)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}6}\; +\; -\; +$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=west] at ($(z7)+(0.20,0.12)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}7}\; +\; +\; +$};
            \node[statelabel, anchor=east] at ($(z0)+(-0.20,-0.02)$) {$\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}0}\; -\; -\; -$};
        \end{tikzpicture}
        \caption{Transition diagram of the elementary switching states of the 2L-VSI}
        \label{fig:transition_diagram_elementary_switching_states}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Alternating SVM with equally distributed zero voltage vectors %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Alternating SVM with equally distributed zero voltage vectors}
    The usual SVM sequence implementation is the \hl{centered and alternating SVM} scheme, which applies the zero vectors $\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}0}$ and $\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}7}$ for equal time at the beginning and end of the \hl{pulse period} $T_\mathrm{p}$. The active vectors are applied in an alternating sequence to minimize switching losses.
    \begin{figure}
        \centering
        \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1.06cm,y=1cm,>=Latex]
            \tikzset{
                statelabel/.style={font=\small, fill=white, inner sep=0.8pt},
                timelabel/.style={fill=white, inner sep=0.4pt}
            }

            \foreach \x in {0,...,8} {
                    \draw[gray!55,dashed] (\x,-1.05) -- (\x,3.45);
                }

            \node[left] at (-0.15,2.55) {$s_\mathrm{a}(t)$};
            \node[left] at (-0.15,1.45) {$s_\mathrm{b}(t)$};
            \node[left] at (-0.15,0.35) {$s_\mathrm{c}(t)$};

            \draw[black,thick] (0,2.30) -- (1,2.30) -- (1,3.00) -- (7,3.00) -- (7,2.30) -- (8,2.30);
            \draw[black,thick] (0,1.20) -- (2,1.20) -- (2,1.90) -- (6,1.90) -- (6,1.20) -- (8,1.20);
            \draw[black,thick] (0,0.10) -- (3,0.10) -- (3,0.80) -- (5,0.80) -- (5,0.10) -- (8,0.10);

            \foreach \x/\state in {
            0.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}0}},
            1.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}},
            2.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}},
            3.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}7}},
            4.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}7}},
            5.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}2}},
            6.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}1}},
            7.5/{\bm{s}_{\mathrm{i}0}}
            } {
            \node[statelabel] at (\x,3.25) {$\state$};
            }

            \foreach \x/\dwlabel in {
            0.5/{\tau_0},
            1.5/{\tau_1},
            2.5/{\tau_2},
            3.5/{\tau_7},
            4.5/{\tau_7},
            5.5/{\tau_2},
            6.5/{\tau_1},
            7.5/{\tau_0}
            } {
            \node[timelabel] at (\x,-0.52) {$\dwlabel$};
            }

            \draw[{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}-{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}, thick] (0,-0.95) -- (4.0,-0.95);
            \draw[{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}-{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}, thick] (4.0,-0.95) -- (8.0,-0.95);
            \node[timelabel] at (2.00,-0.95) {$T_\mathrm{p}$};
            \node[timelabel] at (6.00,-0.95) {$T_\mathrm{p}$};
        \end{tikzpicture}
        \caption{Centered alternating SVM switching pattern (example for first sector).}
        \label{fig:alternating_svm_equal_zero_distribution}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}




%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Centered SVM and equivalence to min-max injection %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{SVM equivalence to PWM with min-max injection}
    \small
    According to \figref{fig:alternating_svm_equal_zero_distribution}, the SVM's average switching state over the pulse period is
    \begin{equation}
        \overline{\bm{s}}_\mathrm{abc}
        =
        \tau_0 \begin{bmatrix} -1 \\ -1 \\ -1 \end{bmatrix}
        +
        \tau_1 \begin{bmatrix} 1 \\ -1 \\ -1 \end{bmatrix}
        +
        \tau_2 \begin{bmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ -1 \end{bmatrix}
        +
        \tau_7 \begin{bmatrix} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{bmatrix}.
    \end{equation}
    For the \hl{symmetric zero-vector distribution} of centered and alternating SVM,
    \begin{equation}
        \tau_0 = \tau_7 = \frac{1-\tau_1-\tau_2}{2}.
    \end{equation}
    Inserting this gives
    \begin{equation}
        \overline{\bm{s}}_\mathrm{abc}
        =
        \begin{bmatrix}
            \tau_1+\tau_2  &
            -\tau_1+\tau_2 &
            -\tau_1-\tau_2
        \end{bmatrix}^\top.
    \end{equation}
    This is exactly the shifted phase reference from the min-max zero-sequence PWM \eqref{eq:shifted_three_phase_PWM_modulation_indices_min_max}:
    \begin{equation}
        \overline{\bm{s}}_\mathrm{abc} = \bm{s}^{**}_\mathrm{abc}.
    \end{equation}
    Hence, \hl{centered and alternating SVM is identical to carrier-based PWM with min-max zero-sequence injection} in the linear range. This statement applies to all sectors due to symmetry.
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Digital control implementation and regular sampling %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

        \label{fig:digital_control_implementation}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Digital control implementation (cont.) %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Digital control implementation (cont.)}
    Inverter control is implemented on digital control plattforms with a \hl{sampling frequency} $f_\mathrm{s}=\nicefrac{1}{T_\mathrm{s}}$ and a \hl{pulse frequency} $f_\mathrm{p}=\nicefrac{1}{T_\mathrm{p}}$. Here, several challenges arise:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Real-time requirement}: The control algorithm incl. I/O must be executed within the sampling period $T_\mathrm{s}$ (typically a few microseconds to a few hundred microseconds).
        \item \textbf{Delay / dead time}: The control period acts a delay / dead time, which can affect the stability and performance of the system if not properly accounted for in the control design.
        \item \textbf{Modulation induced current ripple}: The modulation introduces a ripple in the phase currents. If not properly accounted for, control loops will try to fight this system-immanent ripple, which can deteriorate the control performance.
    \end{itemize}
    \vspace{-0.2cm}
    \begin{varblock}{Regular sampling}
        To overcome the above issues in modulator-based control, regular sampling combines two key features:  \textbf{measurement synchronization} with the modulator to blank out the current ripple and \textbf{reference synchronization} to ensure that sampled control reference values deterministically impact the switching pattern of the next pulse period.
    \end{varblock}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Measurement synchronization via regular sampling: ripple blanking %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Measurement synchronization via regular sampling: ripple blanking}
    \begin{figure}
        \centering
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\numIntervals}{18}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\sampleStep}{360/\numIntervals}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\voltageAmplitude}{0.76}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\currentAmplitude}{0.84}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\loadAngle}{90}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\integrationGain}{pi/180*\currentAmplitude/\voltageAmplitude}
            \pgfmathsetmacro{\currentInitial}{-\currentAmplitude}

            \def\voltageSampleCoords{(0,0)}
            \def\voltageHoldCoords{}
            \def\switchCoords{}
            \def\carrierCoords{}
            \def\currentBaseCoords{(0,\currentInitial)}
            \def\currentPwmCoords{(0,\currentInitial)}
            \def\currentSampleCoords{(0,\currentInitial)}
            \xdef\currentState{\currentInitial}

            \foreach \k in {0,...,17} {
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\thetaLeft}{\k*\sampleStep}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\thetaRight}{(\k+1)*\sampleStep}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\heldVoltage}{\voltageAmplitude*sin(\thetaLeft)}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\currentLeft}{\currentState}
                    \pgfmathtruncatemacro{\parity}{mod(\k,2)}
                    \ifnum\parity=0
                        \pgfmathsetmacro{\switchStart}{1}
                        \pgfmathsetmacro{\switchEnd}{-1}
                        \pgfmathsetmacro{\switchEdge}{\thetaLeft + 0.5*\sampleStep*(1 + \heldVoltage)}
                        \xdef\carrierCoords{\carrierCoords (\thetaLeft,-1) (\thetaRight,1)}
                    \else
                        \pgfmathsetmacro{\switchStart}{-1}
                        \pgfmathsetmacro{\switchEnd}{1}
                        \pgfmathsetmacro{\switchEdge}{\thetaLeft + 0.5*\sampleStep*(1 - \heldVoltage)}
                        \xdef\carrierCoords{\carrierCoords (\thetaLeft,1) (\thetaRight,-1)}
                    \fi
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\currentBaseRight}{\currentLeft + \integrationGain*\heldVoltage*(\thetaRight-\thetaLeft)}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\currentEdge}{\currentLeft + \integrationGain*\switchStart*(\switchEdge-\thetaLeft)}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\currentPwmRight}{\currentEdge + \integrationGain*\switchEnd*(\thetaRight-\switchEdge)}
                    \pgfmathsetmacro{\nextVoltageSample}{\voltageAmplitude*sin(\thetaRight)}

                    \xdef\voltageHoldCoords{\voltageHoldCoords (\thetaLeft,\heldVoltage) (\thetaRight,\heldVoltage)}
                    \xdef\voltageSampleCoords{\voltageSampleCoords (\thetaRight,\nextVoltageSample)}
                    \xdef\switchCoords{\switchCoords (\thetaLeft,\switchStart) (\switchEdge,\switchStart) (\switchEdge,\switchEnd) (\thetaRight,\switchEnd)}
                    \xdef\currentBaseCoords{\currentBaseCoords (\thetaRight,\currentBaseRight)}
                    \xdef\currentPwmCoords{\currentPwmCoords (\switchEdge,\currentEdge) (\thetaRight,\currentPwmRight)}
                    \xdef\currentSampleCoords{\currentSampleCoords (\thetaRight,\currentBaseRight)}
                    \xdef\currentState{\currentBaseRight}
                }

            \begin{groupplot}[
                group style={group size=1 by 2, vertical sep=0.35cm},
                width=0.95\textwidth,
                height=0.52\textheight,
                xmin=0,
                xmax=360,
                xtick distance=\sampleStep,
                xticklabels={},
                axis x line=middle,
                axis y line=middle,
                x axis line style={-{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}},
                ymin=-1.18,
                ymax=1.18,
                ytick={-1,0,1},
                yticklabels={,,},
                enlargelimits=false,
                clip=false,
                tick style={black},
                every axis plot/.append style={line join=round}
                ]
                \nextgroupplot[]
                \addplot[black, thick] coordinates {\carrierCoords};
                \addplot[signalalpha, thick] coordinates {\switchCoords};
                \addplot[signalbeta, dashed, thick, samples=401, domain=0:360] {\voltageAmplitude*sin(x)};
                \addplot[signalbeta, thick] coordinates {\voltageHoldCoords};
                \addplot[signalbeta, only marks, mark=o, mark size=2pt, mark options={draw=signalbeta, fill=white}] coordinates {\voltageSampleCoords};

                \node[signalalpha, fill=white, inner sep=0.8pt] at (axis cs:41,0.8) {$s_\mathrm{a}(t)$};
                \node[fill=white, inner sep=0.8pt] at (axis cs:80,0.26) {$c(t)$};
                \node[signalbeta, fill=white, inner sep=0.8pt] at (axis cs:260,-0.42) {$s_{\mathrm{a},k}^{*}$};
                \node[signalbeta, fill=white, inner sep=0.1pt] at (axis cs:221,-0.80) {$s_\mathrm{a}^{*}(t)$};
                \node[overlay, anchor=west] at (axis description cs:1.01,0.5) {$t$};
                \node[overlay, rotate=90, anchor=south] at (axis description cs:-0.01,0.5) {VSI voltage};



                \nextgroupplot[]
                \addplot[black!55, densely dotted, samples=2, domain=0:360] {0};
                \addplot[signaldelta, thick, dashed] coordinates {\currentBaseCoords};
                \addplot[signaldelta, thick] coordinates {\currentPwmCoords};
                \addplot[signaldelta, only marks, mark=o, mark size=2pt, mark options={draw=signaldelta, fill=white}] coordinates {\currentSampleCoords};



                \node[overlay, anchor=west] at (axis description cs:1.01,0.5) {$t$};
                \node[overlay, rotate=90, anchor=south] at (axis description cs:-0.01,0.5) {Current response};
                \draw[thin] (axis cs:105,0.55) -- (axis cs:125,0.45);
                \node[signaldelta, left, anchor=east, fill=white, inner sep=0.5pt] at (axis cs:105,0.55) {$i_\mathrm{a}(t)$};
                \draw[thin] (axis cs:275,0.64) -- (axis cs:262,0.35);
                \node[signaldelta, align=center, above, fill=white, inner sep=0.5pt] at (axis cs:275,0.64) {$i_{\mathrm{a},k}$};
            \end{groupplot}
        \end{tikzpicture}
        \caption{Zero-order-held voltage samples and ripple-free current sampling}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}



%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% PWM with continuous-time reference values %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}[t]
    \frametitle{PWM with continuous-time and zero-order-hold reference values}
    \begin{figure}
        \centering
        \scriptsize
        \textbf{Continuous-time reference $s^*(t)$}\par
        \vspace{-0.3cm}
        \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1.0cm,y=1.0cm,>=Latex]
            \tikzset{
            waveLabel/.style={fill=white, inner sep=0.8pt},
            axisTick/.style={black, thick},
            periodmark/.style={{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}-{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}, thick},
            carrierWave/.style={black, thick},
            switchWave/.style={signalalpha, thick},
            referenceWave/.style={signalbeta, thick}
            }

            \draw[-{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}, thick] (-0.55,0) -- (8.95,0) node[right] {$t$};
            \draw[-{Latex[length=4pt,width=4pt]}, thick] (0,-1.25) -- (0,1.35);

            \foreach \y/\ylabel in {1/1,-1/-1} {
                    \draw[axisTick] (-0.08,\y) -- (0.08,\y);
                    \node[left] at (-0.14,\y) {$\ylabel$};
                }
            \foreach \x in {1.20,2.40,3.60,4.80,6.00,7.20,8.40} {
                    \draw[axisTick] (\x,0.06) -- (\x,-0.06);
                }

            \draw[carrierWave] (0,1) -- (1.20,-1) -- (2.40,1) -- (3.60,-1) -- (4.80,1) -- (6.00,-1) -- (7.20,1) -- (8.40,-1);

            \draw[switchWave]
            (0,-1) -- (0.92,-1) -- (0.92,1) -- (1.48,1) -- (1.48,-1)
            -- (2.95,-1) -- (2.95,1) -- (4.35,1) -- (4.35,-1)
            -- (5.30,-1) -- (5.30,1) -- (6.84,1) -- (6.84,-1)
            -- (7.48,-1) -- (7.48,1) -- (8.45,1);

                    (5.80,0.20) (6.55,0.32) (7.25,0.50) (8.30,0.75)
                };

            \node[signalalpha, waveLabel, above] at (1.26,1.06) {$s(t)$};
            \node[waveLabel, below] at (2.42,0.52) {$c(t)$};
            \node[signalbeta, waveLabel] at (3.7,0.55) {$s^*(t)$};

            \draw[periodmark] (2.40,-0.35) -- node[fill=white, inner sep=0.8pt] {$T_\mathrm{p}$} (4.80,-0.35);
        \end{tikzpicture}

        \par\vspace{0.1cm}

        \vspace{-0.08cm}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references  %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references}
    The synchronized reference sequence based on regular sampling as illustrated in \figref{fig:synchronized_references_regular_sampling} can be modeled as a sum of time-shifted step functions $\sigma(t)$ as follows:
    \begin{equation}
        s^*(t) = \sum_{k} s_k^* \left[ \sigma\left(t - kT_\mathrm{s}\right) - \sigma\left(t - (k+1)T_\mathrm{s}\right) \right].
    \end{equation}
    Using the Laplace transform of the step function $\mathcal{L}\left\{\sigma(t-t_0)\right\}=\nicefrac{e^{-t_0s}}{s}$, we get:
    \begin{equation}
        S^*(s) = \sum_{k} s_k^* \left[ \frac{e^{-kT_\mathrm{s}s}}{s} - \frac{e^{-(k+1)T_\mathrm{s}s}}{s} \right] = \sum_{k} s_k^*e^{-kT_\mathrm{s}s} \frac{1}{s}\left[1  - e^{-T_\mathrm{s}s} \right].
    \end{equation}
    Here, we can identify two terms:
    \begin{align*}
        S^*(z) & = \sum_{k} s_k^*z^{-k} \quad \text{with} \quad z = e^{T_\mathrm{s}s} \quad \text{(discrete-time reference sequence in the $z$-domain),} \\
        H(s)   & = \frac{1}{T_\mathrm{s}s}\left[1  - e^{-T_\mathrm{s}s} \right] \quad \text{(transfer function of a sample and hold element)}.
    \end{align*}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references (cont.) %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references (cont.)}
    Hence, we can rewrite the Laplace transform of the synchronized reference sequence as follows:
    \begin{equation}
        S^*(s) = \sum_{k} s_k^*e^{-kT_\mathrm{s}s} \frac{1}{s}\left[1  - e^{-T_\mathrm{s}s} \right] = T_\mathrm{s} H(s) S^*(e^{T_\mathrm{s}s}).
    \end{equation}
    The transfer function $H(s)$ represents the impact of the synchronized regular sampling on the control loop. Rewriting $H(s=\mathrm{j}\omega)$, we get:
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{split}
            H(\mathrm{j}\omega) & = \frac{1}{T_\mathrm{s}\mathrm{j}\omega}\left[1 - e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}}\right] = \frac{1}{T_\mathrm{s}\mathrm{j}\omega}e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2}\left[e^{\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2} - e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2}\right] \\
                                & = \frac{2}{T_\mathrm{s}\mathrm{j}\omega}e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2}\sin\left(\frac{\omega T_\mathrm{s}}{2}\right) = e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2}\sinc\left(\frac{\omega T_\mathrm{s}}{2}\right)
        \end{split}
    \end{equation}
    With the angular sampling frequency $\omega_\mathrm{s} = \nicefrac{2\pi}{T_\mathrm{s}}$, we can rewrite $H(\mathrm{j}\omega)$ as follows:
    \begin{equation}
        H(\mathrm{j}\omega) = e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2}\sinc\left(\pi\frac{\omega}{\omega_\mathrm{s}}\right).
        \label{eq:regular_sampling_transfer_function_sinc}
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references (cont.) %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references (cont.)}
    Since the $\sinc(\cdot)$ function can be a little abstract, two frequent approximations can be used. The first one is the \hl{first-order Pad\'e approach} of the exponential function:
    \begin{equation}
        e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}} \approx \frac{1 - \mathrm{j}\omega \nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}}{1 + \mathrm{j}\omega \nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}} \quad \Rightarrow \quad H(\mathrm{j}\omega) \approx \frac{1}{T_\mathrm{s}\mathrm{j}\omega}\left(1-\frac{1 - \mathrm{j}\omega \nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}}{1 + \mathrm{j}\omega \nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}}\right)=\frac{1}{1+\mathrm{j}\omega \nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}}.
    \end{equation}
    This results in a first-order low-pass filter with a time constant of half the sampling time $T_\mathrm{s}$. The second one is derived by a \hl{Taylor series expansion} of the $\sinc(\cdot)$ function
    \begin{equation}
        \sinc\left(\pi\frac{\omega}{\omega_\mathrm{s}}\right) = 1 - \frac{\pi^2}{6}\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_\mathrm{s}}\right)^2 + \frac{\pi^4}{120}\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_\mathrm{s}}\right)^4 - \frac{\pi^6}{5040}\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_\mathrm{s}}\right)^6 + \ldots \approx 1,
    \end{equation}
    which yields:
    \begin{equation}
        H(\mathrm{j}\omega) \approx e^{-\mathrm{j}\omega T_\mathrm{s}/2}.
    \end{equation}
    Hence, this delivers a pure time delay of half the sampling time $T_\mathrm{s}$. In both approximations, \hl{$\nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}$ can be interpreted as the relevant time constant of the synchronized regular sampling}.
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references (cont.) %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Delay model of discrete-time synchronized references (cont.)}
    \begin{figure}
        \centering
        \begin{tikzpicture}
            \def\xmaxnorm{1.999}
            \begin{groupplot}[
                    group style={group size=1 by 2, xticklabels at=edge bottom, vertical sep=0.3cm},
                    width=0.9\textwidth,
                    height=0.45\textheight,
                    xmin=0.1,
                    xmax=2,
                    xmode=log,
                    log basis x=10,
                    domain=0.1:\xmaxnorm,
                    samples=250,
                    grid,
                    xtick={0.1,0.2,0.5,1,2},
                    xticklabels={$0.1$,$0.2$,$0.5$,$1$,$2$},
                    tick label style={font=\scriptsize},
                    label style={font=\scriptsize},
                    legend style={at={(0.5,1.025)}, anchor=south, legend columns=3, draw=none, fill=none, font=\scriptsize},
                ]
                \nextgroupplot[
                    ylabel={Amplitude / dB},
                    ymin=-20,
                    ymax=2,
                    ytick={0,-5,-10,-15,-20},
                ]
                \addplot[black, thick] {20*ln(abs(sin(deg(pi*x))/(pi*x)))/ln(10)};
                \addlegendentry{$H(\mathrm{j}\omega)$}
                \addplot[signalalpha, thick, dash dot] {-10*ln(1 + (pi*x)^2)/ln(10)};
                \addlegendentry{Pad\'e approx.}
                \addplot[signaldelta, thick, dashed] {0};
                \addlegendentry{Taylor approx.}

                \nextgroupplot[
                    ylabel={Phase / deg},
                    xlabel={Normalized frequency $\omega / \omega_\mathrm{s}$},
                    ymin=-190,
                    ymax=10,
                    ytick={0,-45,-90,-135,-180},
                ]
                \addplot[black, thick] {-180*(x-floor(x))};
                \addplot[signalalpha, thick, dash dot] {-atan(pi*x)};
                \addplot[signaldelta, thick, dashed] {-180*x};
            \end{groupplot}
        \end{tikzpicture}
        \caption{Bode plot of different transfer functions representing the reference synchronization as part of the regular sampling approach}
        \label{fig:Bode_transfer_regular_sampling_reference}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Total control delay and compensation %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Total control delay and compensation}
    As already indicated in \figref{fig:digital_control_implementation}, the \hl{effective total control delay is $\nicefrac{3}{2}T_\mathrm{s}$} for a regular sampling digital control implementation. This delay is the sum of two components:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item \textbf{Reference synchronization}: $\nicefrac{T_\mathrm{s}}{2}$ (compare \eqref{eq:regular_sampling_transfer_function_sinc} and the approximations thereof).
        \item \textbf{Controller processing}: $T_\mathrm{s}$ (incl. sensor data acquisition, control algorithm execution, and actuation signal generation).
    \end{itemize}
    While this impact must be considered for every control design, at least one $T_\mathrm{s}$ delay can be compensated by simple \hl{feedforward prediction} of the system's states:
    \begin{equation}
        \hat{\bm{x}}_{k+1} = \bm{f}(\bm{x}_k, \bm{u}_k),
    \end{equation}
    where $\hat{\bm{x}}_{k+1}$ is the predicted state at the next sampling instant, $\bm{x}_k$ is the current state, and $\bm{f}(\cdot)$ represents the system's dynamics. The input $\bm{u}_k$ is the control action from the last controller cycle, e.g., the average reference voltage $\bm{u}_k = \bm{u}_{\mathrm{abc},k}^*$. The next control cycle can then be based on the predicted state $\hat{\bm{x}}_{k+1}$ instead of the current state $\bm{x}_k$.
\end{frame}





%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\subsection{Optimal pulse patterns}
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%


%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Pulse patterns encoded by switching angles %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Pulse patterns encoded by switching angles}
    A two-level \hl{pulse pattern} is described by a binary switching function
    \begin{equation}
        s(t)\in\{-1,+1\},
        \qquad \theta=\omega t,
        \qquad 0\leq \theta<2\pi.
    \end{equation}
    For harmonic analysis, we parameterize the same switching function over the \hl{electrical angle} \(\theta\), which is \hl{synchronized} with the fundamental control frequency. If the pattern has \(M\) commutations within one electrical period, it is characterized by the ordered \hl{switching-angle vector}
    \begin{equation}
        \bm{\alpha}=
        \begin{bmatrix}
            \alpha_1 & \alpha_2 & \ldots & \alpha_M
        \end{bmatrix}^{\top},
        \qquad
        0<\alpha_1<\alpha_2<\ldots<\alpha_M<2\pi.
    \end{equation}

    \begin{figure}
        \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1.15cm,y=0.75cm,>=Latex]
            \draw[->] (0,0) -- (6.62,0) node[right] {$\theta$};
            \draw[->] (0,-1.45) -- (0,1.45) node[left, yshift=4pt] {$s(\theta)$};

            \foreach \x in {1.571,3.142,4.712,6.283} {
                    \draw[thin] (\x,0.1) -- (\x,-0.1);
                }

            \draw[signalalpha, thick]
            (0,1) -- (0.82,1) -- (0.82,-1) -- (1.3,-1) -- (1.3,1) -- (2.42,1) -- (2.42,-1) -- (3.43,-1)
            -- (3.43,1) -- (4.18,1) -- (4.18,-1) -- (5.08,-1) -- (5.08,1) -- (6.283,1);


            \node[above] at (0.82,1.15) {$\alpha_1$};
            \node[below] at (1.3,-1.15) {$\alpha_2$};
            \node[above] at (2.42,1.15) {$\alpha_3$};
            \node[below] at (3.42,-1.15) {$\alpha_4$};
            \node[above] at (4.18,1.15) {$\alpha_5$};
            \node[below] at (5.08,-1.15) {$\alpha_6$};

            \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (1.571,0) {$\nicefrac{\pi}{2}$};
            \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (3.142,0) {$\pi$};
            \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (4.712,0) {$\nicefrac{3\pi}{2}$};
            \node[below, yshift=-2pt, xshift=-2pt] at (6.283,0) {$2\pi$};
            \node[left, xshift=-2pt] at (0,1) {$+1$};
            \node[left, xshift=-2pt] at (0,-1) {$-1$};
        \end{tikzpicture}
        \caption{Generic pulse pattern over one electrical period}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Symmetry constraints%%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Symmetry constraints}
    Symmetry assumptions reduce the number of independent switching angles, i.e., reduce the complexity of the pulse pattern problem.
    \begin{columns}[t,onlytextwidth]
        \begin{column}{0.375\textwidth}

            \par\vspace{0.25em}
            \textbf{Half-wave symmetry (HWS):}
            \[
                s(\theta+\pi)=-s(\theta)
            \]
            the second half wave is obtained from the first half by sign inversion.

            \vspace{1em}
            \textbf{Quarter-wave symmetry (QWS):}
            \[
                s(\theta)=s(\pi-\theta),
                \qquad
                0\leq\theta\leq\frac{\pi}{2},
            \]
            is applied together with HWS.
        \end{column}
        \hfill
        \begin{column}{0.6\textwidth}
            \par\vspace{0.25em}
            \begin{tikzpicture}[x=1.15cm,y=0.75cm,>=Latex]

                \draw[->] (0,0) -- (6.55,0) node[right] {$\theta$};
                \draw[->] (0,-1.42) -- (0,1.48) node[left, xshift=-1pt, yshift=6pt] {$s(\theta)$};

                \foreach \x in {1.571,3.142,4.712,6.283} {
                        \draw[thin] (\x,0.08) -- (\x,-0.08);
                    }

                \draw[signalalpha, thick]
                (0,-1) -- (0.60,-1) -- (0.60,1) -- (1.12,1) -- (1.12,-1) -- (1.571,-1);
                \draw[signalalpha, thick, dashed]
                (1.571,-1) -- (2.022,-1) -- (2.022,1) -- (2.542,1) -- (2.542,-1) -- (3.142,-1)
                -- (3.142,1) -- (3.742,1) -- (3.742,-1) -- (4.262,-1) -- (4.262,1) -- (5.163,1) -- (5.163,-1) -- (5.683,-1) -- (5.683,1) -- (6.283,1);

                \node[font=\scriptsize] at (0.79,1.5) {independent};
                \node[font=\scriptsize] at (2.36,1.5) {mirror by QW};
                \node[font=\scriptsize] at (4.71,1.5) {derived by HW};


                \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (1.571,0) {$\nicefrac{\pi}{2}$};
                \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (3.142,0) {$\pi$};
                \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (4.712,0) {$\nicefrac{3\pi}{2}$};
                \node[below, yshift=-2pt, xshift=-2pt] at (6.283,0) {$2\pi$};
                \node[left, xshift=-2pt] at (0,1) {$+1$};
                \node[left, xshift=-2pt] at (0,-1) {$-1$};
            \end{tikzpicture}
        \end{column}
    \end{columns}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Harmonic coefficients I %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{From the pulse pattern to Fourier coefficients}
    Consider a \hl{HWS} two-level pulse pattern with switching angles in the first half wave:
    \begin{equation}
        0<\alpha_1<\ldots<\alpha_N<\pi,
        \qquad
        s(\theta+\pi)=-s(\theta).
    \end{equation}
    With \(\alpha_0=0\) and \(\alpha_{N+1}=\pi\), the first half wave is
    \begin{equation}
        s_\mathrm{h}(\theta;\bm{\alpha}) = (-1)^i,
        \qquad
        \theta\in[\alpha_i,\alpha_{i+1}),
        \quad i\in\{0,\ldots,N\}.
    \end{equation}
    The evaluate the harmonic spectrum of \(s(\theta)\), we use the Fourier series expansion
    \begin{equation}
        s(\theta)=\frac{a_0}{2}+\sum_{k=1}^{\infty}\left(a_k\cos(k\theta)+b_k\sin(k\theta)\right).
    \end{equation}
    Because of \hl{HWS}, \(a_0=0\) and only odd harmonics remain, i.e., \(a_k=b_k=0\) for even \(k\). Evaluating the Fourier integrals interval by interval gives for HWS, for odd \(k\),
    \begin{equation}
        a_k(\bm{\alpha})=\frac{4}{k\pi}\sum_{i=1}^{N}(-1)^{i+1}\sin(k\alpha_i),
        \qquad
        b_k(\bm{\alpha})=\frac{4}{k\pi}\left(1+\sum_{i=1}^{N}(-1)^i\cos(k\alpha_i)\right).
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Harmonic coefficients II %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Quarter-wave symmetry Fourier coefficients}
    If, in addition, the waveform is \hl{QWS},
    \begin{equation}
        s(\theta)=s(\pi-\theta),
        \qquad
        0\leq\theta\leq\frac{\pi}{2},
    \end{equation}
    then only the first quarter wave must be parameterized:
    \begin{equation}
        \mathcal{A}_{\mathrm{QW}}=
        \left\{
        \bm{\alpha}\in\mathbb{R}^N \;\middle|\;
        0<\alpha_1<\ldots<\alpha_N<\frac{\pi}{2}
        \right\}.
    \end{equation}
    We define that the first quarter wave starts at \(-1\), which, however, is an arbitrary choice:
    \begin{equation}
        s_\mathrm{q}(\theta;\bm{\alpha}) = (-1)^{i+1},
        \qquad
        \theta\in[\alpha_i,\alpha_{i+1}),
        \quad i\in\{0,\ldots,N\},
        \qquad
        \alpha_0=0,\; \alpha_{N+1}=\frac{\pi}{2}.
    \end{equation}
    Then mirror symmetry eliminates the cosine family, so for odd \(k\) the \hl{QWS spectrum is fully described by}
    \begin{equation}
        a_k(\bm{\alpha})=0,
        \qquad
        b_k(\bm{\alpha})=\frac{4}{k\pi}
        \left(-1+2\sum_{i=1}^{N}(-1)^{i+1}\cos(k\alpha_i)\right),
        \qquad
        k\in\{1,3,5,\ldots\}.
        \label{eq:opp_harmonics_rev4}
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% OPP problem %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{The pulse pattern problem as a constrained optimization task}
    Based on the Fourier coefficient description of the pulse pattern, we formulate a general (plant-independent) \hl{optimal pulse pattern (OPP)} problem as
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{aligned}
            \min_{\bm{\alpha}\in\mathcal{A}} \quad &
            J\bigl(\bm{\alpha};m^*\bigr)
            =
            \sum_{\ell\in\mathcal{S}}
            w_\ell\left|\gamma_\ell(\bm{\alpha})\right|^2 \\
            \text{subject to} \quad                &
            \gamma_{\mathrm{fund}}(\bm{\alpha})=m^*,      \\
                                                   &
            c_r\bigl(\bm{\alpha};m^*\bigr)=0,
            \qquad
            r\in\{1,\ldots,n_\mathrm{eq}\},
            \\
                                                   &
            d_q\bigl(\bm{\alpha};m^*\bigr)\leq 0,
            \qquad
            q\in\{1,\ldots,n_\mathrm{ineq}\}.
        \end{aligned}
        \label{eq:general_plant_independent_opp_rev3}
    \end{equation}

    \begin{itemize}
        \item \(\mathcal{A}\) collects angle ordering, chosen symmetries (HW, QW, \dots), and further admissibility constraints.
        \item \(\gamma_\ell\) denotes the selected spectral quantity, e.g. \(a_h\), \(b_h\), or a harmonic magnitude; \(\mathcal{S}\) indexes the weighted spectrum components.
        \item \(\gamma_{\mathrm{fund}}\) is the quantity used to impose the desired fundamental amplitude \(m^*\).
        \item Problem \eqref{eq:general_plant_independent_opp_rev3} is generally non-convex and requires a thoughtful numerical solver selection choice. As it is plant-independent, it can be pre-solved for arbitrary applications.
    \end{itemize}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% SHE principle: HW %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Selective harmonic elimination (SHE): HWS case}
    With half-wave symmetry, the admissible set becomes
    \begin{equation}
        \mathcal{A}_{\mathrm{HWS}}=
        \left\{\bm{\alpha}\in\mathbb{R}^N\;\middle|\;0<\alpha_1<\ldots<\alpha_N<\pi\right\}.
    \end{equation}
    Both coefficient families \(a_h\) and \(b_h\) of odd order may be present, so exact harmonic elimination must be formulated coefficient-wise:
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{aligned}
            \min_{\bm{\alpha}\in\mathcal{A}_{\mathrm{HW}}} \quad & 0                       \\
            \text{subject to} \quad                              & a_1(\bm{\alpha})=0,
            \qquad b_1(\bm{\alpha})=m^*,                                                   \\
                                                                 & a_{h_r}(\bm{\alpha})=0,
            \qquad b_{h_r}(\bm{\alpha})=0,
            \qquad r\in\left\{1,\ldots,\left\lfloor\frac{N-2}{2}\right\rfloor\right\}.
        \end{aligned}
        \label{eq:general_she_hw_rev1}
    \end{equation}
    Here, \(h_r\) are the targeted odd harmonic orders.

    \begin{itemize}
        \item The cost function $J$ is dropped since SHE targets exact elimination, i.e., zero magnitude of the selected harmonics (enforced by the constraints).
        \item Each canceled odd harmonic order \(h_r\) requires two scalar equalities, namely \(a_{h_r}=0\) and \(b_{h_r}=0\), i.e., at most \(\left\lfloor\frac{N-2}{2}\right\rfloor\) odd harmonic orders can be canceled in the HW case.
    \end{itemize}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% SHE principle: QW %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Selective harmonic elimination (SHE): QWS case}
    If quarter-wave symmetry is imposed in addition, then \(\bm{\alpha}\in\mathcal{A}_{\mathrm{QWS}}\) and the cosine family vanishes identically, i.e. \(a_h(\bm{\alpha})=0\). The SHE problem therefore simplifies to
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{aligned}
            \min_{\bm{\alpha}\in\mathcal{A}_{\mathrm{QWS}}} \quad & 0                       \\
            \text{subject to} \quad                               & b_1(\bm{\alpha})=m^*,   \\
                                                                  & b_{h_r}(\bm{\alpha})=0,
            \qquad r\in\{1,\ldots,N-1\}.
        \end{aligned}
        \label{eq:general_she_qw_rev4}
    \end{equation}
    Here, \(h_r\) are the odd harmonic orders to be eliminated, for example \(5,7,11,13,\ldots\).

    \begin{itemize}
        \item QWS symmetry reduces each eliminated harmonic to one scalar constraint only.
        \item Hence, with one fundamental constraint and \(N-1\) harmonic constraints, the number of switching angles \(N\) equals the number of constraints, which is a necessary condition for the existence of a solution.
    \end{itemize}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% SHE solved example %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Selective harmonic elimination (SHE): solved QWS example}
    Consider the QWS SHE problem with \(N=3\) switching angles in the first quarter wave targeting the elimination of the 5th and 7th harmonics:
    \begin{equation}
        m^*=0.8,
        \qquad
        b_5=0,
        \qquad
        b_7=0.
    \end{equation}
    Here, the fundamental reference voltage is an arbitrary value. This yields the equation system
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{aligned}
            \frac{4}{\pi}\left(-1+2\cos(\alpha_1)-2\cos(\alpha_2)+2\cos(\alpha_3)\right)     & = 0.8, \\
            \frac{4}{5\pi}\left(-1+2\cos(5\alpha_1)-2\cos(5\alpha_2)+2\cos(5\alpha_3)\right) & = 0,   \\
            \frac{4}{7\pi}\left(-1+2\cos(7\alpha_1)-2\cos(7\alpha_2)+2\cos(7\alpha_3)\right) & = 0.
        \end{aligned}
    \end{equation}

    One feasible solution is
    \begin{equation}
        \alpha_1=\SI{18.35}{\degree},
        \qquad
        \alpha_2=\SI{37.03}{\degree},
        \qquad
        \alpha_3=\SI{48.45}{\degree}.
    \end{equation}
    In practice, such transcendental SHE equation systems are solved numerically by nonlinear root-finding solvers (e.g., Newton method).
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% SHE example pattern %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

    For
    \begin{equation}

        \qquad

    the resulting pulse pattern is

            \foreach \x in {1.571,3.142,4.712,6.283} {

                }

            \draw[signalalpha, thick]

            \node[below, yshift=-2pt] at (4.712,0) {$\nicefrac{3\pi}{2}$};
            \node[below, yshift=-2pt, xshift=-2pt] at (6.283,0) {$2\pi$};
            \node[left, xshift=-2pt] at (0,1) {$+1$};

        \end{tikzpicture}
        \caption{Exemplary QWS pulse pattern for SHE}
    \end{figure}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Plant-dependent OPP %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Plant-dependent optimized pulse patterns}
    In many applications, the relevant performance variable is not the converter voltage itself but a plant variable.
    Then the OPP problem must include the \hl{plant dynamics} explicitly:
    \begin{equation}
        \dot{\bm{x}}(t)=\bm{f}\bigl(\bm{x}(t),\bm{u}(t)\bigr),
        \qquad
        \bm{y}(t)=\bm{g}\bigl(\bm{x}(t),\bm{u}(t)\bigr),
    \end{equation}
    with the switched converter input
    \begin{equation}
        \bm{u}(t)=\frac{u_\mathrm{dc}(t)}{2}\,\bm{s}(t;\bm{\alpha}).
    \end{equation}
    A general application-agnostic formulation is
    \begin{equation}
        \begin{aligned}
            \min_{\bm{\alpha}\in\mathcal{A}} \quad &
            J=
            \frac{1}{T}
            \int_{0}^{T}
            \ell\bigl(\bm{x}(t),\bm{y}(t),\bm{u}(t),\bm{\alpha}\bigr)\,\mathrm{d}t \\
            \text{subject to} \quad                &
            \dot{\bm{x}}(t)=\bm{f}\bigl(\bm{x}(t),\bm{u}(t)\bigr),                 \\
                                                   &
            \bm{x}(T)=\bm{x}(0),                                                   \\
                                                   &
            c_r\bigl(\bm{x}(\cdot),\bm{y}(\cdot),\bm{\alpha}\bigr)=0,              \\
                                                   &
            d_q\bigl(\bm{x}(\cdot),\bm{y}(\cdot),\bm{\alpha}\bigr)\leq 0.
        \end{aligned}
        \label{eq:general_plant_dependent_opp_rev3}
    \end{equation}
\end{frame}

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
%% Plant-dependent OPP applications %%
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\begin{frame}
    \frametitle{Plant-dependent optimized pulse patterns: example applications}
    In \eqref{eq:general_plant_dependent_opp_rev3}, the cost function \(J\) is defined as a time average of a stage cost \(\ell\), which may address:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item load-current mean-square-error reduction,
        \item torque-ripple or acoustic-noise reduction,
        \item power loss reduction within the plant and/or converter.
    \end{itemize}
    Further, the constraints \(c_r\) and \(d_q\) may enforce:
    \begin{itemize}
        \item periodicity of the state trajectory,
        \item admissible switching angle ranges,
        \item maximum harmonic magnitudes,
        \item upper power loss limits, etc.
    \end{itemize}
    \begin{varblock}{Key idea}
        The optimization problem is no longer formulated directly on the converter behavior alone, but on the plant response generated by a converter switching pattern.
    \end{varblock}
\end{frame}
